{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01NHpjujSYeBq9ZoJiEWV1oR","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# `client-go` Architecture\n     2→\n     3→This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n     4→major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n     5→\n     6→## Client Configuration\n     7→\n     8→There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n     9→`rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n    10→`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n    11→complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n    12→authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n    13→\n    14→## REST Client\n    15→\n    16→The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n    17→the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n    18→Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n    19→\n    20→The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n    21→chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n    22→task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n    23→is injected into requests.\n    24→\n    25→The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n    26→response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n    27→supporting different client models from a common base.\n    28→\n    29→### Endpoint Interactions\n    30→\n    31→*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n    32→    (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n    33→    request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n    34→    Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n    35→*   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n    36→    object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n    37→    `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n    38→*   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n    39→    return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n    40→    responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n    41→    Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n    42→*   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n    43→    `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n    44→    objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n    45→    the underlying streaming protocol.\n    46→\n    47→### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n    48→\n    49→*   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n    50→    `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n    51→*   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n    52→    `WarningHandler`.\n    53→*   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n    54→    client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n    55→*   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n    56→    responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n    57→\n    58→## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n    59→\n    60→To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n    61→models.\n    62→\n    63→The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n    64→\n    65→The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n    66→to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n    67→1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n    68→    **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n    69→2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n    70→    resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n    71→\n    72→## Code Generation\n    73→\n    74→A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n    75→strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n    76→controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n    77→several key components:\n    78→\n    79→*   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n    80→*   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n    81→*   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n    82→*   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n    83→\n    84→A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n    85→of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n    86→\n    87→`sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n    88→\n    89→## Controller Infrastructure\n    90→\n    91→The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n    92→request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n    93→\n    94→The data flow is as follows:\n    95→\n    96→```mermaid\n    97→graph TD\n    98→    subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n    99→        API_Server[API Server]\n   100→    end\n   101→\n   102→    subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n   103→        Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n   104→        DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n   105→        Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n   106→        EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n   107→    end\n   108→\n   109→    subgraph \"User Code\"\n   110→        WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n   111→        Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n   112→    end\n   113→\n   114→    API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n   115→    Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n   116→    DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n   117→    Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n   118→    EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n   119→    WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n   120→    Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n   121→```\n   122→\n   123→A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n   124→`resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n   125→stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n   126→**\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n   127→`Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n   128→`resourceVersion` to restart from.\n   129→\n   130→The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n   131→logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n   132→\n   133→## Controller Patterns\n   134→\n   135→The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n   136→ensure resiliency.\n   137→\n   138→The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n   139→and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n   140→work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n   141→blocking the informer's watch stream.\n   142→\n   143→For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n   144→solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n   145→shared `Lease` object.\n   146→\n   147→## Server-Side Apply\n   148→\n   149→`client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n   150→server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n   151→model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n   152→**`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n   153→construct the declarative patch.\n   154→\n   155→## Versioning and Compatibility\n   156→\n   157→`client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n   158→version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n   159→\n   160→The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n   161→version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. It describes the\nmajor components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n\n## Client Configuration\n\nThere is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n`rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n`tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\ncomplex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\nauthentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n\n## REST Client\n\nThe `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\nthe low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\nKubernetes-specific object logic.\n\nThe `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\nchain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\ntask, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\nis injected into requests.\n\nThe client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\nresponse processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\nsupporting different client models from a common base.\n\n### Endpoint Interactions\n\n*   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n    (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n    request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n    Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n*   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n    object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n    `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n*   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n    return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n    responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n    Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n*   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n    `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n    objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n    the underlying streaming protocol.\n\n### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n\n*   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n    `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n*   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n    `WarningHandler`.\n*   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n    client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n*   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n    responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n\n## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n\nTo handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\nmodels.\n\nThe **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n\nThe **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\nto interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n    **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n    resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n\n## Code Generation\n\nA core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\nstrongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\ncontroller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\nseveral key components:\n\n*   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n*   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n*   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n*   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n\nA contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\nof these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n\n`sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n\n## Controller Infrastructure\n\nThe `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\nrequest-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n\nThe data flow is as follows:\n\n```mermaid\ngraph TD\n    subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n        API_Server[API Server]\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n        Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n        DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n        Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n        EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n    end\n\n    subgraph \"User Code\"\n        WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n        Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n    end\n\n    API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n    Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n    DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n    Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n    EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n    WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n    Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n```\n\nA **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n`resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\nstream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n**\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n`Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n`resourceVersion` to restart from.\n\nThe **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\nlogic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n\n## Controller Patterns\n\nThe controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\nensure resiliency.\n\nThe **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\nand reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\nwork queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\nblocking the informer's watch stream.\n\nFor high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\nsolution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\nshared `Lease` object.\n\n## Server-Side Apply\n\n`client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\nserver's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\nmodel that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n**`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\nconstruct the declarative patch.\n\n## Versioning and Compatibility\n\n`client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\nversion `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n\nThe Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\nversion of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\nA contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n","numLines":163,"startLine":1,"totalLines":163}}}

However, everything they share in common (i.e., most APIs)\n  will work.\n\nSee the [CHANGELOG](./CHANGELOG.md) for a detailed description of changes\nbetween client-go versions.\n\n| Branch         | Canonical source code location      | Maintenance status |\n| -------------- | ----------------------------------- | ------------------ |\n| `release-1.25` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.25 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.26` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.26 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.27` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.27 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.28` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.28 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.29` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.29 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.30` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.30 branch   | =-                 |\n| `release-1.31` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.31 branch   | =                  |\n| `release-1.32` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.32 branch   | ✓                  |\n| `release-1.33` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.33 branch   | ✓                  |\n| `release-1.34` | Kubernetes main repo, 1.34 branch   | ✓                  |\n| client-go HEAD | Kubernetes main repo, master branch | ✓                  |\n\nKey:\n\n* `✓` Changes in main Kubernetes repo are actively published to client-go by a bot\n* `=` Maintenance is manual, only severe security bugs will be patched.\n* `-` Deprecated; please upgrade.\n\n#### Deprecation policy\n\nWe will maintain branches for at least six months after their first stable tag\nis cut. (E.g., the clock for the release-2.0 branch started ticking when we\ntagged v2.0.0, not when we made the first alpha.) This policy applies to\nevery version greater than or equal to 2.0.\n\n#### Why do the 1.4 and 1.5 branch contain top-level folder named after the version?\n\nFor the initial release of client-go, we thought it would be easiest to keep\nseparate directories for each minor version. That soon proved to be a mistake.\nWe are keeping the top-level folders in the 1.4 and 1.5 branches so that\nexisting users won't be broken.\n\n### Kubernetes tags\n\nThis repository is still a mirror of\n[k8s.io/kubernetes/staging/src/client-go](https://github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes/tree/master/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go),\nthe code development is still done in the staging area.\n\nSince Kubernetes `v1.8.0`, when syncing the code from the staging area,\nwe also sync the Kubernetes version tags to client-go, prefixed with\n`kubernetes-`. From Kubernetes `v1.17.0`, we also create matching semver\n`v0.x.y` tags for each `v1.x.y` Kubernetes release.\n\nFor example, if you check out the `kubernetes-1.17.0` or the `v0.17.0` tag in\nclient-go, the code you get is exactly the same as if you check out the `v1.17.0`\ntag in Kubernetes, and change directory to `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go`.\n\nThe purpose is to let users quickly find matching commits among published repos,\nlike [sample-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/sample-apiserver),\n[apiextension-apiserver](https://github.com/kubernetes/apiextensions-apiserver),\netc. The Kubernetes version tag does NOT claim any backwards compatibility\nguarantees for client-go. Please check the [semantic versions](#versioning) if\nyou care about backwards compatibility.\n\n### How to get it\n\nTo get the latest version, use go1.16+ and fetch using the `go get` command. For example:\n\n```\ngo get k8s.io/client-go@latest\n```\n\nTo get a specific version, use go1.11+ and fetch the desired version using the `go get` command. For example:\n\n```\ngo get k8s.io/client-go@v0.20.4\n```\n\nSee [INSTALL.md](/INSTALL.md) for detailed instructions and troubleshooting.\n\n### How to use it\n\nIf your application runs in a Pod in the cluster, please refer to the\nin-cluster [example](examples/in-cluster-client-configuration), otherwise please\nrefer to the out-of-cluster [example](examples/out-of-cluster-client-configuration).\n\n### Dependency management\n\nFor details on how to correctly use a dependency management for installing client-go, please see [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md).\n\n### Contributing code\nPlease send pull requests against the client packages in the Kubernetes main [repository](https://github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes). {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01DGnE6Z4AyapHmv5EmucySC","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→/*\n     2→Copyright 2015 The Kubernetes Authors.\n     3→\n     4→Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n     5→you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n     6→You may obtain a copy of the License at\n     7→\n     8→    http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n     9→\n    10→Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n    11→distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n    12→WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n    13→See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n    14→limitations under the License.\n    15→*/\n    16→\n    17→package cache\n    18→\n    19→import (\n    20→\t\"context\"\n    21→\t\"errors\"\n    22→\t\"fmt\"\n    23→\t\"sync\"\n    24→\t\"sync/atomic\"\n    25→\t\"time\"\n    26→\n    27→\t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/api/meta\"\n    28→\t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/runtime\"\n    29→\tutilruntime \"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/runtime\"\n    30→\t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/wait\"\n    31→\t\"k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/synctrack\"\n    32→\t\"k8s.io/utils/buffer\"\n    33→\t\"k8s.io/utils/clock\"\n    34→\t\"k8s.io/utils/ptr\"\n    35→\n    36→\t\"k8s.io/klog/v2\"\n    37→\n    38→\tclientgofeaturegate \"k8s.io/client-go/features\"\n    39→)\n    40→\n    41→// SharedInformer provides eventually consistent linkage of its\n    42→// clients to the authoritative state of a given collection of\n    43→// objects.  An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\n    44→// namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\n    45→// an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\n    46→// SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\n    47→// group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\n    48→// SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\n    49→// applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\n    50→//\n    51→// The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\n    52→// access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\n    53→// An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\n    54→// other appropriate content or (2) \"absent\".\n    55→//\n    56→// A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\n    57→// by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\n    58→// machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\n    59→// the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\n    60→// consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\n    61→// prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\n    62→// particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\n    63→// then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\n    64→// eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\n    65→// state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\n    66→// service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\n    67→// absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\n    68→// selector.\n    69→//\n    70→// For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\n    71→// states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\n    72→// states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\n    73→// might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\n    74→// states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\n    75→// ordering between states seen for different objects.\n    76→//\n    77→// The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\n    78→// during `Run()`.\n    79→//\n    80→// As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\n    81→// unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\n    82→// collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\n    83→// SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\n    84→// collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\n    85→// service ends, or communication problems between the two\n    86→// persistently thwart achievement).\n    87→//\n    88→// As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\n    89→// non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\n    90→// removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\n    91→// removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\n    92→// too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\n    93→// problems persistently thwart the desired result).\n    94→//\n    95→// The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\n    96→// objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\n    97→// Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\n    98→// a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\n    99→// into its constituent parts.\n   100→//\n   101→// Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\n   102→// snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\n   103→// will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\n   104→//\n   105→// A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\n   106→// update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\n   107→// added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\n   108→// stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\n   109→// after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\n   110→// additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\n   111→// client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\n   112→// local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\n   113→// SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\n   114→// update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\n   115→// update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\n   116→// corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\n   117→// and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\n   118→// For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\n   119→// delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\n   120→// object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n   121→// `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n   122→// that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n   123→// `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n   124→// and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n   125→// created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n   126→// rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\n   127→// need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\n   128→// field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\n   129→// notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\n   130→//\n   131→// A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\n   132→// is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\n   133→// notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\n   134→// to something else, for example through a\n   135→// `client-go/util/workqueue`.\n   136→//\n   137→// A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\n   138→// state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\n   139→// ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\n   140→type SharedInformer interface {\n   141→\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\n   142→\t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\n   143→\t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\n   144→\t// different handlers.\n   145→\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\n   146→\t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\n   147→\t// seen every item in the initial list).\n   148→\t//\n   149→\t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\n   150→\tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\n// namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\n// an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\n// SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\n// group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\n// SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\n// applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\n//\n// The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\n// access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\n// An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\n// other appropriate content or (2) \"absent\".\n//\n// A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\n// by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\n// machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\n// the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\n// consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\n// prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\n// particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\n// then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\n// eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\n// state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\n// service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\n// absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\n// selector.\n//\n// For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\n// states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\n// states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\n// might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\n// states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\n// ordering between states seen for different objects.\n//\n// The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\n// during `Run()`.\n//\n// As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\n// unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\n// collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\n// SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\n// collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\n// service ends, or communication problems between the two\n// persistently thwart achievement).\n//\n// As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\n// non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\n// removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\n// removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\n// too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\n// problems persistently thwart the desired result).\n//\n// The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\n// objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\n// Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\n// a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\n// into its constituent parts.\n//\n// Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\n// snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\n// will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\n//\n// A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\n// update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\n// added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\n// stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\n// after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\n// additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\n// client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\n// local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\n// SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\n// update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\n// update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\n// corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\n// and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\n// For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\n// delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\n// object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n// `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n// that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n// `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n// and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n// created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n// rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\n// need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\n// field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\n// notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\n//\n// A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\n// is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\n// notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\n// to something else, for example through a\n// `client-go/util/workqueue`.\n//\n// A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\n// state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\n// ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\ntype SharedInformer interface {\n\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\n\t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\n\t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\n\t// different handlers.\n\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\n\t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\n\t// seen every item in the initial list).\n\t//\n\t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\n\tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)","numLines":150,"startLine":1,"totalLines":1144}}}
